But since we need arbitrary offsets, let's compute: plane_stride = H * W plane_start = (b * C + c) * plane_stride cur = x[plane_start + h*W + w] w0 = weight[c*3 + 0] w1 = weight[c*3 + 1] w2 = weight[c*3 + 2] bval = bias[c] // LR pred_lr = (h*W + w > 0) ? x[plane_start + h*W + w - 1] : 0 succ_lr = (h*W + w + 1 < H*W) ? x[plane_start + h*W + w + 1] : 0 lr = bval + w0*pred_lr + w1*cur + w2*succ_lr // RL int addr_rl_pred, addr_rl_succ; if (w < W - 1) addr_rl_pred = plane_start + h*W + w + 1; else if (h > 0) addr_rl_pred = plane_start + (h-1)*W + 0; else addr_rl_pred = -1; if (w > 0) addr_rl_succ = plane_start + h*W + w - 1; else if (h < H - 1) addr_rl_succ = plane_start + (h+1)*W + (W-1); else addr_rl_succ = -1; pred_rl = (addr_rl_pred >= 0) ? x[addr_rl_pred] : 0; succ_rl = (addr_rl_succ >= 0) ? x[addr_rl_succ] : 0; rl = bval + w0*pred_rl + w1*cur + w2*succ_rl; // TB int addr_tb_pred, addr_tb_succ; if (h > 0) addr_tb_pred = plane_start + (h-1)*W + w; else if (w > 0) addr_tb_pred = plane_start + (H-1)*W + (w-1); else addr_tb_pred = -1; if (h < H - 1) addr_tb_succ = plane_start + (h+1)*W + w; else if (w < W - 1) addr_tb_succ = plane_start + 0*W + (w+1); else addr_tb_succ = -1; pred_tb = (addr_tb_pred >= 0) ? x[addr_tb_pred] : 0; succ_tb = (addr_tb_succ >= 0) ? x[addr_tb_succ] : 0; tb = bval + w0*pred_tb + w1*cur + w2*succ_tb; // BT int addr_bt_pred, addr_bt_succ; if (h < H - 1) addr_bt_pred = plane_start + (h+1)*W + w; else if (w > 0) addr_bt_pred = plane_start + 0*W + (w-1); else addr_bt_pred = -1; if (h > 0) addr_bt_succ = plane_start + (h-1)*W + w; else if (w < W - 1) addr_bt_succ = plane_start + (H-1)*W + (w+1); else addr_bt_succ = -1; pred_bt = (addr_bt_pred >= 0) ? x[addr_bt_pred] : 0; succ_bt = (addr_bt_succ >= 0) ? x[addr_bt_succ] : 0; bt = bval + w0*pred_bt + w1*cur + w2*succ_bt; out[plane_start + h*W + w] = cur + lr + rl + tb + bt; Wait, actually in the reference, `acc` starts at zeros and then each scan adds its conv result. The residual is `x + acc`. So each scan contributes `Conv(scan)`. In my kernel: `out = x + (lr + rl + tb + bt)`. Is that correct? Let's compute for a single point: scan LR: bval + w0*pred_lr + w1*cur + w2*succ_lr scan RL: bval + w0*pred_rl + w1*cur + w2*succ_rl etc. Sum of four biases = 4*bval. In the reference, each scan applies the conv with its own bias. So yes, total bias added is 4*bias. But wait, is that what the reference actually does? Reference: `acc = torch.zeros_like(x)` `acc = acc + self._apply_scan_conv_unscan(x)` # LR `acc = acc + self._apply_scan_conv_unscan(xr).flip(...)` # RL `acc = acc + yt` # TB `acc = acc + yb` # BT `return x + acc` Yes, each of the four conv1d calls includes bias addition. So total bias contribution is 4*bias per channel. My kernel computes exactly that: 4 * bval + contributions from weights. Let's also verify the weight application: For scan LR, the conv at position seq[i] uses input[i-1], input[i], input[i+1]. That's correct. For scan RL, in the reference: xr is flipped, conv is applied, then flipped back. For position (h,w): In xr, value is x[h,W-1-w]. Conv output at xr position (h, w0=W-1-w) uses xr[h, w0-1], xr[h, w0], xr[h, w0+1] (with boundaries padded). This is: x[h, W-w0], x[h, W-1-w0], x[h, W-2-w0] = x[h, w+1], x[h, w], x[h, w-1]. Wait, but after flipping back, the output at original position (h,w) corresponds to conv output at xr position (h, W-1-w), which uses xr neighbors at (h, W-2-w), (h, W-1-w), (h, W-w). These are x[h, w+1], x[h, w], x[h, w-1]. The weights are applied in the order w0 to left neighbor, w1 to center, w2 to right neighbor. In the xr sequence, left neighbor is w0-1 (sequence index smaller), which corresponds to original w+1. Right neighbor is w0+1, original w-1. So the conv output is: bias + w0 * x[h, w+1] + w1 * x[h, w] + w2 * x[h, w-1]. Note the weight order is reversed relative to the original spatial order because the sequence is reversed. Wait, is this correct? Let's think about it carefully. Conv1d with kernel [w0, w1, w2] on a 1D sequence s: output[i] = w0*s[i-1] + w1*s[i] + w2*s[i+1]. For the reversed sequence s_rev[j] = s[N-1-j], the conv output at j is: out_rev[j] = w0 * s_rev[j-1] + w1 * s_rev[j] + w2 * s_rev[j+1] = w0 * s[N-1-(j-1)] + w1 * s[N-1-j] + w2 * s[N-1-(j+1)] = w0 * s[N-j] + w1 * s[N-1-j] + w2 * s[N-2-j]. When we flip the output back: out[i] = out_rev[N-1-i] (where i is the original index). Let j = N-1-i. Then: out[i] = w0 * s[N - (N-1-i)] + w1 * s[N-1-(N-1-i)] + w2 * s[N-2-(N-1-i)] = w0 * s[i+1] + w1 * s[i] + w2 * s[i-1]. Ah! So after flip-conv-unflip, the effective kernel is reversed: [w2, w1, w0]. Let's verify with a small example: s = [a, b, c] w = [w0, w1, w2] s_rev = [c, b, a] conv(s_rev) with pad=1: [w0*0+w1*c+w2*b, w0*c+w1*b+w2*a, w0*b+w1*a+w2*0] flip back: [w0*b+w1*a+w2*0, w0*c+w1*b+w2*a, w0*0+w1*c+w2*b] For position 0 (a): w0*b + w1*a + w2*0. This uses right neighbor b with weight w0 and center a with w1. So yes, reversed: [w2, w1, w0] applied to original: w2*pad + w1*a + w0*b. This is a critical detail! My RL calculation above used the SAME weights [w0, w1, w2] for the reversed neighbors. But it should use REVERSED weights [w2, w1, w0] because the flip reverses the spatial order. Let's re-examine my earlier derivation: I said for RL at (h,w) where w0 = W-1-w: predecessor in sequence j-1 corresponds to original w+1. I applied weight w0 to it. But the flip-conv-unflip means the effective weight for original w+1 should be w2. Let's re-derive: Original sequence (LR): index i, value s[i]. Conv output: w0*s[i-1] + w1*s[i] + w2*s[i+1]. For RL (flip W): The sequence is s_rev where s_rev[j] = s[W-1-j] within each row. But the reference uses flatten(2) on the flipped (H,W) tensor, which means it flattens both H and W dimensions in row-major order of the flipped tensor. Within a row of the flipped tensor, the sequence is indeed reversed. But what about across rows? Since the flipped tensor is still row-major, flattening it gives row1_rev followed by row2_rev, etc. The key insight is: for a 2D tensor, flip(-1) then flatten(2) does NOT simply reverse the entire H*W sequence. It reverses each row individually and then concatenates them. This is different from reversing the whole flattened sequence. Wait, flatten(2) with dims=(-2,-1) flattens starting from dim 2. For a tensor of shape (H, W), flatten(2) gives a sequence of length H*W where elements are ordered: (0,0), (0,1), ..., (0,W-1), (1,0), ..., (1,W-1), ... . For the flipped tensor xr where xr[h,w] = x[h, W-1-w], the flattened sequence is: xr[0,0], xr[0,1], ..., xr[0,W-1], xr[1,0], ... = x[0,W-1], x[0,W-2], ..., x[0,0], x[1,W-1], ... So each row is reversed, but the row order is preserved. Now, for the conv1d applied to this sequence: for an element at position j in the sequence, its neighbors j-1 and j+1 are within the same row (if not at row boundaries). For the first element of a row (j=h*W), j-1 is the last element of the previous row. So the conv spans across rows! Because flatten(2) makes the entire 2D grid into a single 1D sequence. This means my earlier analysis for RL was correct in terms of sequence indices, but the WEIGHT reversal issue only applies if the entire sequence is reversed. When rows are individually reversed but then concatenated, the conv1d operates on the concatenated sequence. For a boundary between rows j=h*W and j-1=h*W-1, the conv at j uses j-1 (last element of previous row) as the "left" neighbor with weight w0, and j+1 (second element of current row) as the "right" neighbor with weight w2. But for the flip-conv-unflip operation: we flip W, apply conv on the flattened sequence, then flip W on the result. Let's trace the effective mapping for a specific position. Position (h, w) in original x. In xr (flip W), its corresponding position is (h, W-1-w). Flattened index in xr: j = h*W + (W-1-w). Conv output at j: w0 * xr_flat[j-1] + w1 * xr_flat[j] + w2 * xr_flat[j+1] (with padding). Unflip W on the output: output at original (h,w) = conv_output at xr position (h, W-1-w). Let's compute xr_flat[j-1] and xr_flat[j+1]: xr_flat[j] = xr[h, W-1-w] = x[h, w]. xr_flat[j-1]: If W-1-w > 0 (i.e., w < W-1): xr_flat[j-1] = xr[h, W-1-w-1] = x[h, w+1]. If W-1-w == 0 (i.e., w == W-1): j-1 = h*W - 1. This is xr[h-1, W-1] = x[h-1, 0] (if h>0). xr_flat[j+1]: If W-1-w < W-1 (i.e., w > 0): xr_flat[j+1] = xr[h, W-1-w+1] = x[h, w-1]. If W-1-w == W-1 (i.e., w == 0): j+1 = h*W + W. This is xr[h+1, 0] = x[h+1, W-1] (if h < H-1). So the conv output at original (h,w) is: w0 * (neighbor corresponding to j-1) + w1 * x[h,w] + w2 * (neighbor corresponding to j+1). But does this equal reversing the kernel? Let's compare: My earlier "full reverse" analysis said the effective kernel for a fully reversed sequence is [w2, w1, w0]. But here, the sequence is NOT fully reversed; it's reversed within each row, and rows are concatenated. Within a row, yes, the spatial order is reversed. But at row boundaries, the conv connects the last element of the previous reversed row to the first element of the current reversed row. In the original flattened sequence (LR), at row boundary j=h*W, the predecessor is the last element of the previous row: x[h-1, W-1]. The successor is the first element of the current row: x[h, 0]. In the RL flattened sequence, at row boundary j=h*W (which corresponds to xr[h,0] = x[h, W-1]), the predecessor is xr[h-1, W-1] = x[h-1, 0]. The successor is xr[h, 1] = x[h, W-2]. So the RL scan's conv uses DIFFERENT cross-row neighbors than the LR scan. This is intended by the reference. Now, for the weight reversal question: within a row, the sequence is reversed. So for elements not at row boundaries, the conv output at (h,w) with w<W-1 and w>0 uses: w0 * x[h, w+1] + w1 * x[h, w] + w2 * x[h, w-1]. In the reference's _apply_scan_conv_unscan for RL: xr = x.flip(dims=(-1,)) y = self.dw_conv(xr.flatten(2)).view(B,C,H,W) result = y.flip(dims=(-1,)) The conv output at xr position (h, W-1-w) uses xr neighbors. Since xr is flipped, xr[h, W-1-w-1] = x[h, w+1], and xr[h, W-1-w+1] = x[h, w-1]. But the conv kernel weights are fixed: w0 for left neighbor, w1 for center, w2 for right neighbor in the INPUT sequence. Since the input sequence to the conv is the reversed row, the "left" neighbor in the sequence corresponds to the original spatial right neighbor (higher w), and the "right" neighbor corresponds to the original spatial left neighbor (lower w). So the effective weights for the original spatial neighbors are: original right neighbor (w+1): weight w0 original center (w): weight w1 original left neighbor (w-1): weight w2 But wait, the reference doesn't "reverse the weights". The weights are applied to the sequence elements as they appear in the conv input. Since the input sequence elements at positions j-1, j, j+1 correspond to original x[h, w+1], x[h, w], x[h, w-1], the weights are w0, w1, w2 applied to those respectively. Is this the same as reversing the kernel and applying to the original order? Let's see: reversed kernel [w2, w1, w0] applied to original [w-1, w, w+1]: w2 * x[w-1] + w1 * x[w] + w0 * x[w+1]. This is EXACTLY the same as w0 * x[w+1] + w1 * x[w] + w2 * x[w-1]! Yes, reversing the input sequence is equivalent to reversing the kernel weights when applied to the original sequence. But in my calculation, I was applying the SAME weights [w0, w1, w2] to the neighbors as they appear in the reversed sequence, which gives w0*x[w+1] + w1*x[w] + w2*x[w-1]. This is indeed the correct result for the reference. So my earlier confusion was unfounded. The weights applied to the REVERSED-SEQUENCE neighbors as I computed them are correct. The kernel doesn't need explicit weight reversal. Now, for performance: This kernel has terrible memory access patterns because each thread reads 9 elements (1 center + 4 preds + 4 succs, though shared) from global memory with scattered addresses. For H=32, W=32, B=4, C=64, total elements = 4*64*32*32 = 262144. With 256 threads per block, we need ~1024 blocks. On H100 this is very small. However, the reference also has poor memory access and does 4 separate conv1d operations which involve multiple kernel launches, memory reshaping, and flip/permutations. Our single fused kernel may still be faster due to reduced overhead and better fusion. But we can optimize further. We can load a tile of x into shared memory and compute multiple outputs cooperatively. However, for H=32, W=32, each "scan" is effectively a 1D conv over sequences of length 1024, and there are 4 such scans per channel. Using shared memory for 32x32 tiles would help locality, especially for the cross-row/cross-column accesses. Let's think about a more optimized approach: For each (B, C) plane: - LR and RL are row-wise operations. Within each row of 32 elements, we need left and right neighbors. - TB and BT are column-wise operations. Within each column of 32 elements, we need top and bottom neighbors. If we load a 32x32 tile into shared memory, each thread can easily access its neighbors in all four directions. A 32x32 tile of fp32 is 4KB. With 64 channels and batch 4, we have 256 planes. If we process one plane per block, we can load the tile into shared memory and compute all 4 conv outputs for that tile. But wait, the sequences are FLATTENED over the entire H*W, not just within rows/columns. For LR, the sequence is H*W long, and neighbors wrap across rows. For TB, the sequence is also H*W long (but in column-major interpretation of the permuted tensor), and neighbors wrap across columns. So shared memory for just a 32x32 tile won't be sufficient unless we also include halo elements for the wrap-around boundaries, which are at the edges of the tile. Actually, since H=32 and W=32, a tile of the full plane IS the full plane. We can process the entire plane within a block if we use enough threads. 32*32=1024 elements per plane. With 256 threads per block, we'd need 4 iterations per plane. Or with 1024 threads per block, one plane per block. 1024 threads is fine on H100 (max 1024 per block). But we have B*C = 256 planes. So we'd need 256 blocks of 1024 threads, which is 262144 threads total. That's reasonable. Actually, to maximize parallelism, we can process multiple planes per block or multiple blocks per plane. But shared memory is per block, and loading one 32x32 tile (4KB) is cheap. The main issue is that each element needs to be read by multiple threads (the neighbors). With 1024 threads per block each reading one element, and then accessing neighbors from shared memory, we'd need 32x32 shared memory. Plus, since the scans wrap around, we don't need halos beyond the plane boundaries because the plane IS the full H*W. Wait, the "wrap around" in my logic was due to the flattened sequence structure. For LR, at the boundary between rows (end of row h and start of row h+1), the predecessor of the first element of row h+1 is the last element of row h. So I do need row-to-row access. If the tile is the entire plane, shared memory contains the entire plane and row boundaries are internal to shared memory. Good. Similarly for TB, at the boundary between columns (end of column w and start of column w+1), the predecessor of the first element of column w+1 is the last element of column w. Since the tile is the full plane, shared memory contains all columns. So the simplest optimization is: - Launch one block per (b, c) plane. - Load the entire HxW plane into shared memory. - Compute all four scans using shared memory for all accesses. - Each thread computes one element's output. However, this limits occupancy if we use 1024 threads per block. Let's calculate: - B=4, C=64 -> 256 blocks. - Each block has 1024 threads. - H100 has 132 SMs (or similar for the specific model). With 1024 threads per block and max 1536 threads per SM (on Hopper? Actually, H100 has 2048 threads per SM), we can run 2 blocks per SM concurrently if there are enough registers. 256 blocks total means full GPU utilization. But actually, H=32, W=32, B*C=256. Total threads = 256*1024 = 262,144. Each thread does a few loads and arithmetic operations. This should be very fast, limited by memory bandwidth (reading x once, writing out once) because shared memory helps with the neighbor reads. Let's refine the kernel to use shared memory. turn 4llm latency: 16.0s
reasoning
The ImportError persists. It may be due to the LD_LIBRARY_PATH not containing PyTorch's lib directory when the dynamic linker resolves the .so. Setting LD_LIBRARY_PATH explicitly through os.environ before invoking load_inline can influence the subprocess that compiles and links, but the ultimate import is done by Python's import machinery which respects os.environ. Let's set LD_LIBRARY_PATH and perhaps also LD_PRELOAD. Also remove the unnecessary ctypes; just update env var. We must call load_inline before defining ModelNew, so set env var at the very top.

Actually\n // if h==H-1, next column element at same logical position is w+1 at h=0, memory offset = -(H-1)*W +1.\n scalar_t pred_tb, succ_tb;\n if (h > 0) {\n pred_tb = x[plane_start + (h - 1) * W + w];\n } else if (w > 0) {\n pred_tb = x[plane_start + (H - 1) * W + (w - 1)];\n } else {\n pred_tb = scalar_t(0);\n }\n if (h < H - 1) {\n succ_tb = x[plane_start + (h + 1) * W + w];\n } else if (w < W - 1) {\n succ_tb = x[plane_start + 0 * W + (w + 1)];\n } else {\n succ_tb = scalar_t(0);\n }\n scalar_t tb = bval + w0 * pred_tb + w1 * cur + w2 * succ_tb;\n\n // BT: flip H, permute then flatten. Since TB moves down column (+W)...\n // In BT, logical predecessor (scan move forward) corresponds in original memory to ???\n // More intuitive: flip H => row index reversed. The output at position `i` (0-indexed) is computed as: `sum_{d=0}^{K-1} weight[d] * input_padded[i * stride + d]`. For stride=1, dilation=1, padding=1: `out[i] = weight[0] * padded[i] + weight[1] * padded[i+1] + weight[2] * padded[i+2]`? Wait, the kernel size is 3. If the kernel is aligned such that `weight[0]` is at the leftmost position of the receptive field, then `weight[0]` multiplies `padded[i]` (which corresponds to input[i-1] because of left padding=1), `weight[1]` multiplies `padded[i+1]` (input[i]), `weight[2]` multiplies `padded[i+2]` (input[i+1]). Yes, this matches `w0*pred + w1*cur + w2*succ`. However, is the kernel direction different for RL/TB/BT because of how PyTorch Conv1d views the sequence? PyTorch Conv1d always applies cross-correlation in the forward direction of the sequence. The sequence order determines what "left" and "right" mean relative to the scan direction. For RL: the sequence is reversed rows. The Conv1d processes this sequence forward. So `weight[0]` multiplies the element "before" in the reversed sequence, which is to the right in original row. `weight[2]` multiplies the element "after" in the reversed sequence, which is to the left in original row. Yes, because when `x` is flipped horizontally, the original right neighbor becomes the left neighbor in the sequence. The Conv1d applies `w0` to the left neighbor in the sequence, which is the original right neighbor. Then the output is flipped back. When flipped back, spatial positions are mirrored, but the CONVOLVED values are already assigned to the correct output positions. Since Conv1d is linear and shift-invariant (except boundaries), and then the output is flipped back, the mapping is correct: `out_RL[h,w]` receives contributions from `xr[h, w_rev]` and its neighbors. `xr[h, w_rev] = x[h, W-1-w_rev]`. So yes, `w0*x[h,w+1]` in `out_RL[h,w]`. My code does exactly that. So the formula `bval + w0*pred + w1*cur + w2*succ` where pred is i-1 and succ is i+1 in the sequence is CORRECT for all scans. PyTorch docs: `out[N_i, C_out_j] = bias[C_out_j] + sum_k (weight[C_out_j, k] * input[N_i, k])` where the sum is over kernel positions.
